reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Full Mamba block: RMSNorm -> linear expansion into two branches -> causal depthwise Conv1d ->
    SiLU -> selective scan (input-dependent SSM) -> elementwise gate -> linear projection -> residual.
    This is the complete Mamba architecture block as described in Gu & Dao (2023), used in Mamba,
    Caduceus (bidirectional DNA), and various protein/genomic language models.

    Args:
        d_model (int): Model dimension.
        d_state (int): SSM state dimension (N).
        d_conv (int): Causal convolution kernel size.
        expand (int): Expansion factor for inner dimension.
    """
    def __init__(self, d_model: int, d_state: int, d_conv: int, expand: int):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.d_model = d_model
        self.d_inner = d_model * expand
        self.d_state = d_state
        self.d_conv = d_conv

        self.norm = nn.RMSNorm(d_model)
        self.in_proj = nn.Linear(d_model, self.d_inner * 2, bias=False)
        self.conv1d = nn.Conv1d(self.d_inner, self.d_inner, d_conv,
                                padding=d_conv - 1, groups=self.d_inner, bias=True)
        self.activation = nn.SiLU()

        self.A_log = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(self.d_inner, d_state))
        self.proj_B = nn.Linear(self.d_inner, d_state, bias=False)
        self.proj_C = nn.Linear(self.d_inner, d_state, bias=False)
        self.proj_delta = nn.Linear(self.d_inner, self.d_inner, bias=True)
        self.D = nn.Parameter(torch.ones(self.d_inner))

        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(self.d_inner, d_model, bias=False)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        B, L, D = x.shape
        residual = x
        x = self.norm(x)

        xz = self.in_proj(x)
        x_branch, z = xz.chunk(2, dim=-1)

        x_branch = x_branch.transpose(1, 2)
        x_branch = self.conv1d(x_branch)[..., :L]
        x_branch = x_branch.transpose(1, 2)
        x_branch = self.activation(x_branch)

        N = self.d_state
        delta = torch.nn.functional.softplus(self.proj_delta(x_branch))
        B_t = self.proj_B(x_branch)
        C_t = self.proj_C(x_branch)

        A = -torch.exp(self.A_log)
        dA = torch.exp(delta.unsqueeze(-1) * A.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0))
        dB = delta.unsqueeze(-1) * B_t.unsqueeze(2)
        x_db = x_branch.unsqueeze(-1) * dB

        h = torch.zeros(B, self.d_inner, N, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype)
        outputs = []
        for t in range(L):
            h = dA[:, t] * h + x_db[:, t]
            y_t = (h * C_t[:, t].unsqueeze(1)).sum(dim=-1)
            outputs.append(y_t)
        y = torch.stack(outputs, dim=1)
        y = y + x_branch * self.D.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0)

        z = self.activation(z)
        y = y * z
        y = self.out_proj(y)
        y = y + residual
        return y

batch_size = 16
d_model = 256
d_state = 16
d_conv = 4
expand = 2
seq_len = 2048

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, d_model)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [d_model, d_state, d_conv, expand]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel to replace the forward computation of the Mamba block described. The main goal is to produce numerically equivalent output (fp32), and run faster than the PyTorch reference implementation. Let me analyze the reference implementation to understand the operations and identify the bottleneck. The model is a full Mamba block with: 1. RMSNorm 2. Linear projection (`in_proj`) to `d_inner * 2` 3. Chunk into two branches: `x_branch` and `z` 4. Causal depthwise Conv1d 5. SiLU activation 6. Discretization steps (`delta`, `B_t`, `C_t` projections, `A_log`, compute `dA`, `dB`, etc.) 7. Selective scan (SSM) - a sequential for-loop over `seq_len` (L=2048) - this is likely the main bottleneck 8. `y = y + x_branch * D` (skip connection) 9. Gate: `z = silu(z)`, `y = y * z` 10. Output projection (`out_proj`) 11. Residual add Given the batch size is 16, d_model=256, expand=2 => d_inner=512, d_state=16, seq_len=2048. The sequential SSM loop is the main performance issue. PyTorch's sequential loop over L=2048 in Python is very slow on GPU. We need to fuse as much as possible and implement a custom CUDA kernel for the selective scan. Wait, the batch size, d_inner, d_state, and seq_len are moderate. Could we compute the SSM sequentially per element but in CUDA? Or can we parallelize across batch and d_inner? The selective scan is intrinsically sequential in time for each channel (b, d), but each channel is independent. Since `d_inner = 512` and `batch_size = 16`, we have 8192 independent sequences. Each sequence length is 2048. The state dimension is 16. So for each (b, d) channel, we have a state vector of size N=16, and we do a recurrence: h_t = dA_{b,d,t} * h_{t-1} + x_db_{b,d,t} where dA is scalar per (b,d,t), and x_db is vector of size N. Then y_{b,d,t} = dot(h_t, C_{b,d,t}). This is a standard first-order linear recurrence (like an SSM scan). We can compute this efficiently using a parallel associative scan (Blelloch scan) if the recurrence is linear. However, the scalar multiplier changes per time step. Actually, h_t = a_t * h_{t-1} + u_t, where a_t is scalar and u_t is vector. This is a scalar-vector recurrence. It can be computed with a scan where the associative operator is: Given (a1, u1) and (a2, u2), the composition is (a2*a1, a2*u1 + u2). Wait, let's check: h1 = a1 * h0 + u1 h2 = a2 * h1 + u2 = a2*(a1*h0 + u1) + u2 = (a2*a1)*h0 + (a2*u1 + u2) So the associative tuple is (a, u) where a is scalar, u is vector, and composition is (a2, u2) ∘ (a1, u1) = (a2*a1, a2*u1 + u2). This is a generalized scan (also called cumprod/scan or parallel scan). On CUDA, we can implement a Blelloch-style parallel scan across the sequence length for each (b, d) channel. However, the state dimension N=16 is small, and the vector u_t = x_db_{b,d,t,:} is of size 16. The scan operator is on tuples (scalar a, vector u of size N). If we do a parallel scan across L=2048, for each of 8192 channels, that might be efficient. But also, we could process each sequence serially in a CUDA thread, processing 16 elements (state) with 16 float operations per time step. L=2048, N=16 => 32768 multiply-adds per channel. 8192 channels => ~268 M operations. At memory bandwidth, we need to read/write a lot. Wait, 8192 channels * 2048 time steps * (a few floats) = 8192*2048*~5 floats ~ 84M floats ~ 336 MB. This is manageable. But writing a parallel scan in CUDA is complex. However, given L=2048, which is a power of 2, we could also just have each thread serially process one (b, d) channel. With 8192 channels, we need at least 8192 threads, which is about 128 warps (32 threads/warp). On a modern GPU with many SMs, that's maybe 2-3 warps per SM if we have many SMs. Not enough to hide latency if each thread does a long serial loop of 2048 steps? But 2048 steps with N=16 is 32k operations. On GPU, each thread can do this. With 8192 threads, total work is large. But the occupancy might be low because each thread uses many registers? Let's see. Actually, we can factor the computation: - Compute `xz = in_proj(x)` (matmul) - Split into x_branch, z - Conv1d - SiLU - Compute delta, B, C via linear projections - Compute A, dA, dB, x_db - Run selective scan - y = y + x_branch * D - z = SiLU(z) - y = y * z - out_proj - residual add The linear projections `in_proj`, `proj_B`, `proj_C`, `proj_delta`, `out_proj` are standard matmuls. The conv1d is a depthwise conv with kernel size 4 and padding=3 (causal). `proj_delta` adds a bias. `softplus`. Fusing the entire block into one kernel would be ideal, but writing that much code in CUDA is huge. We can leave the linear layers to PyTorch's efficient cuBLAS, but fuse the selective scan and the surrounding elementwise ops. However, we have a limit: we can only replace the forward computation. The reference implementation uses `nn.Module` layers. In `ModelNew`, we can still use `nn.Linear` etc., but we need to replace the forward. We can call cuBLAS-based PyTorch ops for matmul/conv, and then write a custom CUDA kernel for the selective scan and the elementwise ops around it, to avoid moving data back and forth? Or maybe fuse as much as possible. But the prompt says "Replace the forward computation ... with a custom CUDA kernel." This suggests that the core of the model, especially the selective scan, should be a custom kernel. We can still use PyTorch ops where they are well-optimized, but the main speedup will come from a custom kernel for the scan. Actually, the reference forward calls individual PyTorch ops. We can rewrite the forward to call our own kernel for the SSM part, perhaps fusing the discretization and scan. But maybe the best approach is to implement the entire forward in CUDA? That would minimize memory traffic. Let's see which approach yields the best speedup. The reference implementation uses: - RMSNorm (pytorch native, fast) - in_proj (linear, cuBLAS) - chunk (view) - transpose + conv1d + transpose (depthwise conv, groups=d_inner) - SiLU (fast) - proj_delta + softplus (linear + elementwise) - proj_B (linear) - proj_C (linear) - elementwise compute for A_log, dA, dB, x_db (many small ops, probably memory bound) - sequential for-loop SSM (very slow) - add D - SiLU(z) + gate + out_proj + residual Memory traffic to compute dA, dB, x_db separately is a lot. If we can fuse them into the SSM kernel, we save memory bandwidth. A custom CUDA kernel that takes x_branch (B, L, d_inner), delta (B, L, d_inner), B_t (B, L, d_state?), wait: - delta: (B, L, d_inner) - B_t: (B, L, d_state) - C_t: (B, L, d_state) - A_log: (d_inner, d_state) - D: (d_inner) - x_branch: (B, L, d_inner) And outputs y: (B, L, d_inner) We can compute A = -exp(A_log) on CPU or in a small kernel. Then dA = exp(delta * A) can be computed in the kernel on-the-fly. dB = delta * B_t. Since B_t depends on time but not on d_inner? Wait, B_t is (B, L, d_state). proj_B projects from d_inner to d_state. So for each (b,l), B_t is a vector of size d_state. dB_{b,l,d,n} = delta_{b,l,d} * B_{b,l,n}. This is an outer product across d_inner and d_state. So dB is (B, L, d_inner, d_state). That's a large tensor: 16*2048*512*16 = 268M floats = 1GB. Similarly x_db = x_branch.unsqueeze(-1) * dB is also 1GB. And dA is 16*2048*512*16 = another 268M floats? Wait, dA = exp(delta * A). A is (d_inner, d_state). delta is (B, L, d_inner). So delta.unsqueeze(-1) * A.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0) -> (B, L, d_inner, d_state). That's 268M floats. The reference does this explicitly with broadcasting. That's a lot of memory and intermediate computation. But in the kernel, we can compute these on-the-fly without storing the full 4D tensors. The recurrence is: h_{b,d,0} = 0 (init) for t in 0..L-1: a = exp(delta_{b,t,d} * A_{d,n}) # scalar per (b,t,d,n) u = x_branch_{b,t,d} * delta_{b,t,d} * B_{b,t,n} # per (b,t,d,n) h_{b,d,n} = a * h_{b,d,n} + u y_{b,t,d} = sum_n h_{b,d,n} * C_{b,t,n} So for each (b,d), we have a state vector h[n] of size N=16. Per time step: a_n = exp(delta * A_{d,n}) for n=0..N-1 u_n = x * delta * B_{b,t,n} for n=0..N-1 h_n = a_n * h_n + u_n y += h_n * C_{b,t,n} Note that a_n depends on d and n but not on b,t except through delta. And u_n depends on b,t,d,n. So the main operation per (b,d) channel is a simple sequential loop of length L, each step doing vector operations of size N=16. This is perfect for a CUDA thread: each thread handles one (b,d) pair, loops over t from 0 to L-1, updates h[16], computes y_t. Number of (b,d) pairs: B * d_inner = 16 * 512 = 8192. 8192 threads is relatively few, but each thread does ~L * N * few flops = 2048 * 16 * ~10 = 327k flops. Total flops is about 2.7 GFLOPs? Let's compute: - per (b,d,t): N multiplies for a, N multiplies for u, N fma for h update, N multiplies for dot product, N-1 adds for dot product. But actually, h update and dot product can be partially fused. Let's say ~4N ops per (b,d,t). 8192*2048*64 = ~1.07 billion ops. 1 GFLOP. That's tiny for GPU. But the memory bandwidth: We need to read: - delta: B*L*d_inner = 16*2048*512 = 16.7M floats - x_branch: same = 16.7M - B_t: B*L*d_state = 16*2048*16 = 524k floats - C_t: same = 524k - A_log: d_inner*d_state = 8192 floats - D: d_inner = 512 floats And write: - y: B*L*d_inner = 16.7M floats Total data traffic if perfectly fused: ~3 * 16.7M + 2 * 0.5M = ~51M floats = ~200 MB. At 1 TB/s bandwidth, that's 0.2 ms. Plus compute overhead. The reference's sequential loop in Python is likely orders of magnitude slower. Wait, the reference also has the sequential loop python overhead: loop of 2048 iterations, each doing torch ops on large tensors. That will be very slow. The custom kernel will be easily 100x+ faster just from removing the Python loop. But we also have the overhead of PyTorch layer ops before the scan. Can we fuse more? We can have the custom kernel handle from x_branch onwards (including delta, B_t, C_t computation? No, those come from linear layers). To minimize memory traffic, we could fuse `softplus` and the scan into one kernel. Inside the kernel, for each (batch, channel) pair, we compute a sequential recurrence over L. Let's define the grid: each block handles some channels. The main speedup is avoiding the Python loop. But we also have the issue of reading delta, B_t, C_t, z, x_conv. These are 2D/3D tensors. If each thread reads its own channel's data, memory access is strided. Let's check the layout: - x_conv: shape (B, L, d_inner). The channel dimension is the last dimension. So x_conv[b, t, d] is contiguous in d? Wait, if it's (B, L, d_inner), then d is the fastest-changing dimension. So for a fixed (b,d), the stride to the next t is d_inner. For a fixed (b,t), the d elements are contiguous. - delta: shape (B, L, d_inner), same layout. - B_t: shape (B, L, d_state). d_state=16 is contiguous. - C_t: same. - z: shape (B, L, d_inner). So if we assign thread index `idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x`, and `idx` corresponds to `(b, d)`, then for each time step t, the thread accesses: - x_ptr = x_conv + b*L*d_inner + t*d_inner + d This is a strided access pattern across threads within a warp: thread with d=0 reads addr ..., d=1 reads addr+1. So for a fixed (b,t), threads in a warp read contiguous floats (if the block has threads varying in d). This is perfectly coalesced. So we should group threads by (b,d). Let `total_channels = B * d_inner`. Let `tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x`. If `tid >= total_channels`, return. Then `b = tid / d_inner`, `d = tid % d_inner`. Then loop over t=0..L-1. Inside the loop: - Read x = x_conv[b, t, d] - Read del = delta[b, t, d] - Read z_val = z[b, t, d] (for gating) - Read B_n for n=0..N-1: B_t[b, t, n] - Read C_n for n=0..N-1: C_t[b, t, n] - Read A_d_n for n=0..N-1: A_log[d, n] (precomputed as A = -exp(A_log)). Actually A is (d_inner, d_state). We can precompute `A = -torch.exp(self.A_log)` and store it as a parameter? The reference computes `A = -torch.exp(self.A_log)` each forward. We can do that in the kernel: read `A_log`, compute `A = -exp(A_log)` once per thread and store in registers? There are N=16 values per channel. That's fine. Actually, the standard stable softplus is: ``` softplus(x) = log(1 + exp(x)) for x <= threshold softplus(x) = x for x > threshold ``` with threshold typically around 20. If we compute this in the kernel for each t, it's extra computation. For 2048 steps, that's 2048 log(1+exp(x)) computations per thread. In the PyTorch version, it's done once as a vectorized op. Moving it into the kernel might be slightly slower due to register pressure, but would save 64MB of memory traffic (reading and writing delta). Let me write the kernel: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void selective_scan_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ delta, const float* __restrict__ B, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ A_log, const float* __restrict__ D_param, float* __restrict__ y, int batch, int L, int d_inner, int d_state) { const int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int total = batch * d_inner * d_state; if (tid >= total) return; const int n = tid % d_state; const int tmp = tid / d_state; const int d = tmp % d_inner; const int b = tmp / d_inner; const float A_val = -expf(A_log[d * d_state + n]); const float D_val = D_param[d]; float h = 0.0f; const int x_base = b * L * d_inner + d; const int state_base = b * L * d_state + n; const int y_base = b * L * d_inner + d; for (int t = 0; t < L; ++t) { const float x_val = x[x_base + t * d_inner]; const float delta_val = delta[x_base + t * d_inner]; const float B_val = B[state_base + t * d_state]; const float C_val = C[state_base + t * d_state]; h = expf(delta_val * A_val) * h + x_val * delta_val * B_val; float val = h * C_val; // Reduce within 16-lane group unsigned mask = 0xFFFFFFFF; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset, 16); } const int lane = threadIdx.x & 0x1F; if (lane == 0 || lane == 16) { y[y_base + t * d_inner] = val + x_val * D_val; } } } torch::Tensor selective_scan_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor delta, torch::Tensor B, torch::Tensor C, torch::Tensor A_log, torch::Tensor D_param) { auto batch = x.size(0); auto L = x.size(1); auto d_inner = x.size(2); auto d_state = B.size(2); auto y = torch::empty_like(x); const int total_threads = batch * d_inner * d_state; const int threads_per_block = 256; const int blocks = (total_threads + threads_per_block - 1) / threads_per_block; selective_scan_kernel<<<blocks, threads_per_block>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), delta.data_ptr<float>(), B.data_ptr<float>(), C.data_ptr<float>(), A_log.data_ptr<float>(), D_param.data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), batch, L, d_inner, d_state); return y; } ``` Wait, I should check: `B.size(2)` - B is (B, L, d_state). Time: small because depthwise conv is O(L*K*d_inner) = 16*2048*4*512 ≈ 67M ops. - activation: 64MB read+write - proj_delta: (32768, 512) @ (512, 512) = largest matmul. ~67M * 2 ≈ 134M MACs. Fast. - softplus: 64MB - dA materialization: reads delta, A; writes 1024MB. huge! - dB materialization: reads delta, B; writes 1024MB. huge! - x_db materialization: reads x_branch, dB; writes 1024MB. huge! - Python loop: for 2048 iterations, each does a few elementwise ops on (16, 512, 16) tensors. Even if each iteration takes 0.05ms, total is 100ms. Plus Python/Torch overhead per iteration. So the Python loop is likely 50-200ms, and the tensor materializations might add some memory overhead but probably not the main bottleneck compared to the Python loop. My kernel: the scan + all intermediate math in one go, ~196MB memory traffic plus 268M exps. Time estimate: memory bound at 0.06ms + compute bound at exp: 268M * ~10 cycles / (132SM * 2048 threads * frequency). This is hard to estimate but probably in the 0.2-1ms range. So I expect a huge speedup, probably 50-500x. Let me also think about whether to process the scan in tiles to improve cache locality. The access patterns: - For a given (b,d), the loop over t accesses x[b, t, d], delta[b, t, d], B[b, t, n], C[b, t, n], y[b, t, d]. - These are strided accesses with stride d_inner or d_state in the t dimension. Actually, for fixed (b,d), x[b,0,d], x[b,1,d], x[b,2,d], ... are separated by d_inner floats in memory. That's a stride of 512*4 = 2048 bytes. That's quite large. The cache line on H100 is 128 bytes. So each load pulls in x[b, t, d:d+31] or so. But the next access x[b, t+1, d] is 2048 bytes away, which is 16 cache lines away. Sequential accesses for the same (b,d) strided by 2048 bytes have poor spatial locality. However, since we're processing many (b,d) in parallel, the memory controller can hide some of this. Let me write this kernel: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define D_STATE 16 __global__ void selective_scan_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ delta, const float* __restrict__ B, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ A_log, const float* __restrict__ D_param, float* __restrict__ y, int batch, int L, int d_inner) { const int d_state = D_STATE; const int d_idx = threadIdx.x; const int n = threadIdx.y; const int num_d_per_block = blockDim.x; // 32 const int bd_block = blockIdx.x * num_d_per_block; const int bd = bd_block + d_idx; const int d = bd % d_inner; const int b = bd / d_inner; // Precompute A const float A_val = -expf(A_log[d * d_state + n]); const float D_val = D_param[d]; float h = 0.0f; // Shared memory for reduction: [n][d_idx] extern __shared__ float smem[]; // We'll use smem[n * 32 + d_idx] for (int t = 0; t < L; ++t) { float x_val = x[(b * L + t) * d_inner + d]; float delta_val = delta[(b * L + t) * d_inner + d]; float B_val = B[(b * L + t) * d_state + n]; float C_val = C[(b * L + t) * d_state + n]; h = expf(delta_val * A_val) * h + x_val * delta_val * B_val; smem[n * num_d_per_block + d_idx] = h * C_val; __syncthreads(); // Reduction: first warp handles all d_idx // Actually, let's have the thread with n=0 do the sum for each d_idx if (n == 0) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < d_state; ++i) { sum += smem[i * num_d_per_block + d_idx]; } y[(b * L + t) * d_inner + d] = sum + x_val * D_val; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, `extern __shared__ float smem[];` requires dynamic shared memory allocation. So thread d_idx reads from bank d_idx. No bank conflicts! Great. Actually, the bank index is (address_in_bytes / 4) % 32. Wait, but what if warp n=0 is swapped out while doing the reduction, and another warp from the same SM takes over and finishes t+1? No, warps from the same block all share the same program counter and instruction sequence in the SIMT model. Even if warp n=0 is stalled on memory, the other warps in the block still need to reach the same barrier before any warp can proceed. The standard warp-level reduction using __shfl_down_sync works when ALL threads in the warp participate and the mask covers all of them. To avoid divergence, we can compute mask dynamically: ``` unsigned mask = (lane < 16) ? 0x0000FFFF : 0xFFFF0000; unsigned lane_in_group = lane & 0xF; // lane % 16 for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { float other = __shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset, 16); if (lane_in_group < offset) val += other; } ``` Now all threads in the warp execute the same instructions! Block configuration revisited: - Each block handles 32 (b,d) pairs (one per threadIdx.x) - Each block has 16 warps (one per n=threadIdx.y) - blockDim = (32, 16) = 512 threads - Total blocks = ceil(8192 / 32) = 256 blocks Per iteration t: 1. Each thread loads B[b,t,n] and C[b,t,n] (broadcast-ish, since all threads in a warp share the same n, but different warps have different n. If a warp has 32 threads with: - threadIdx.x = 0..31 (different d) - threadIdx.y = n (same n across the warp) Then at time t: - All threads in the warp load x[b,t,d] for d=0..31: coalesced! - All threads load delta[b,t,d] for d=0..31: coalesced! - All threads load B[b,t,n] (same n): broadcast! - All threads load C[b,t,n] (same n): broadcast! - Each thread computes h for its (b,d,n) - Each thread writes val = h*C to smem[n*32 + d] Then after __syncthreads(), we need to sum over n for each d. We have 256 threads in a block. For the reduction, we want each of 32 threads (d=0..31) to sum 16 values from smem[0..15][d]. How do we do this efficiently? We can use the fact that after __syncthreads(), all threads are synchronized. Then we can assign the first 32 threads (threadIdx.y=0) to do the reduction for all d. Each thread sums 16 elements: ``` if (threadIdx.y == 0) { float sum = 0; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < d_state; ++i) { sum += smem[i * 32 + threadIdx.x]; } y[b, t, d] = sum + x_val * D_val; } ``` But threadIdx.x in threadIdx.y=0 is just 0..31. Each of these 32 threads handles one d value. They read from smem[i*32 + d] for i=0..15. For consecutive d, the reads within the same i are strided by 32 floats = 128 bytes. Bank conflicts? Each thread reads from a different bank (bank = address % 32). smem[i*32 + d] at address (i*32+d)*4. bank = (i*32 + d) % 32 = d % 32. So threads 0..31 all read from different banks for the same i! No bank conflicts! Then 32 threads write 32 adjacent y values: y[b, t, d:d+31]. Coalesced! This is excellent. One issue: the threads with threadIdx.y != 0 do nothing during the reduction. They just wait. But they participate in __syncthreads() before and after. Total __syncthreads() per t: 2. Can I reduce this to 1? If I double-buffer smem, I can eliminate one sync. But let's first implement with 2 syncs. Actually, I can reduce to 1 sync by not having a second sync! As I analyzed earlier, with all threads in the block executing the same loop, the maximum iteration gap is 1. If I use double-buffered smem, threads at t+1 write to buffer[(t+1)%2] while threads at t do reduction. The t+1 write doesn't interfere with the t reduction because they're different buffers, and the max gap of 1 means t+2 won't overwrite buffer[t%2] before t reduction is done. Wait, t+2 would write to buffer[t%2]. By the time t+2 happens, is t guaranteed to be done? After __syncthreads() at the end of iteration t: - All threads arrive. - Threads proceed to t+1. - t+1 writes to buffer[(t+1)%2]. - No __syncthreads(). Threads immediately proceed to compute t+2. - But some threads might still be doing the t reduction! Can they start t+2? No, because after t+1 writes, they don't hit __syncthreads() until the end of t+1. Wait, without a __syncthreads() between t and t+1's compute, threadIdx.y=0 might lag behind because it does the t reduction. ThreadIdx.y=1 proceeds to t+1 compute, then to t+1 write to smem, then to t+1 sync. ThreadIdx.y=0 eventually finishes t reduction, then proceeds to t+1 compute, then t+1 write, then arrives at t+1 sync. Threads with y!=0 WAIT at t+1 sync until y=0 catches up. So threadIdx.y!=0 cannot get to t+2 until threadIdx.y=0 has finished t+1. By that time, threadIdx.y=0 has long finished the t reduction. Safe! So with only 1 __syncthreads() at the end of each iteration, no double buffer needed! The key insight is: threadIdx.y=0 does the reduction AFTER the __syncthreads() but BEFORE the next iteration's compute. Other threads proceed immediately to the next iteration's compute. But they can't write to smem for the next iteration until they've computed. And they can't get past the end of that next iteration until ALL threads catch up. So threadIdx.y=0 is guaranteed to have finished the previous iteration's reduction before any thread writes to smem for t+2. Wait, but what about reading smem? If threadIdx.y=0 reads smem for reduction, can it still be reading while threadIdx.y=1 writes to smem for t+1? ThreadIdx.y=1 writes to smem addresses `smem[n*32 + d]`. ThreadIdx.y=0 reads from `smem[i*32 + d]`. Are these different addresses? Yes, if n != i. But threadIdx.y=1 writes to smem[1*32 + d]. ThreadIdx.y=0 reads from smem[0*32 + d], smem[1*32 + d], ..., smem[15*32 + d]. So they DO conflict on smem[1*32 + d]! Specifically, threadIdx.y=0 reads from n=1 while threadIdx.y=1 writes to n=1. Can this happen simultaneously? ThreadIdx.y=0 is doing the t reduction. ThreadIdx.y=1 is doing t+1 computation and trying to write to smem for t+1. But threadIdx.y=1 can't write to smem for t+1 until it reaches the write instruction. That happens after loading x, delta, B, C, computing exp, etc. During that time, threadIdx.y=0 might still be doing the reduction. If threadIdx.y=1 writes to smem[1*32 + d] while threadIdx.y=0 reads from it, that's a race! To prevent this, we need the second __syncthreads() OR double buffering. Actually, if we add `__syncthreads()` after the reduction, then threadIdx.y=0 waits there, and threadIdx.y=1 must also reach it. But threadIdx.y=1 is doing t+1 compute. Then threadIdx.y=1 proceeds to... wait, if the __syncthreads() is inside the loop, it acts as the end-of-iteration barrier for iteration t. Both y=0 and y=1 must reach it before proceeding to t+1. Wait, I think the timeline is: Loop iteration t: 1. y=1 (and all y!=0): compute, write smem 2. __syncthreads() [Barrier A] 3. y=0: read smem, reduce, write y 4. (loop back) 5. y=1: compute, write smem [for t+1] 6. __syncthreads() [Barrier B] 7. y=0: read smem, reduce, write y [for t+1] At Barrier A, y=1 waits for y=0 to arrive. But y=0 arrives AFTER step 3. During step 3, y=1 has already passed Barrier A and is at step 5 for t+1! This is what we were trying to avoid by not having Barrier A. But WITH Barrier A, y=1 cannot proceed to step 5 until y=0 arrives at Barrier A. Once y=0 arrives, both proceed. This means y=1 doesn't start t+1 until y=0 finishes the t reduction. Safe! So WITH a __syncthreads() after the smem write (Barrier A), y=1 CANNOT race with y=0's reduction because y=1 is blocked at Barrier A until y=0 finishes. Without Barrier A (only one barrier at the end): 1. y=1: compute, write smem 2. (no barrier) 3. y=0: read smem, reduce, write y 4. (no barrier) 5. y=1: compute, write smem for t+1 ... At step 5, y=1 might overwrite smem while y=0 is still at step 3. Race! Then y=1 proceeds to t+1 only after y=0 finishes reduction. This would mean restructuring: compute, reduce/write within the same phase, then barrier. Threads n=0..15 write to smem[n*32 + d]. For iteration t+1, they write to the same locations. Can we have threadIdx.y=n compute t+1 and store to a different "page"? We'd need double buffering: pages 0 and 1, alternating. But even with double buffering, for this race, we'd need y=0 to read from page[t%2] while y=1 writes to page[(t+1)%2]. Wait, that IS what double buffering provides! - Iteration t: all threads write to page0. - Sync. - y=0 reads page0 for reduction. - Meanwhile, y=1 is free to proceed to t+1 and write to page1. They don't conflict because page0 != page1. - At the end of t+1, y=1 writes to page1. - Sync. - y=0 reads page1 for reduction. With double buffering, the two barriers naturally separate the reads from the writes to the same page. And we can remove the barrier after the reduction! Wait, do we still need a barrier after the reduction? After y=0 reads page0 and writes y, the next thing is t+1. At t+1, all threads (including y=0) compute and write to page1. This requires a barrier BEFORE writing to page1 for t+1 to ensure all threads have finished reading page0. But the barrier at the END of t handles that! Actually, let me trace with double buffer and 1 barrier: - t=0: all threads write page0. - Barrier. - y=0 reads page0, writes y. - y=1 computes t=1 and writes to page1 (since page0 is still being read by y=0). Hmm, the barrier at the end of each write phase is necessary to ensure the reduction has started/finished. With 2 barriers: - Write page[t%2] - Barrier - Reduce from page[t%2] - Barrier The second barrier ensures all reductions are done before the next write phase overwrites any page. With double buffer and 1 barrier: - Write page[t%2] - Barrier - Reduce from page[t%2] - (loop) - Write page[(t+1)%2] - Barrier - Reduce from page[(t+1)%2] Since there's a barrier between the write to page[(t+1)%2] and the reduction from page[(t+1)%2], the reduction from page[t%2] is guaranteed to be done before the write to page[t%2] for t+2. Because the t+2 write happens after the t+1 barrier, which all threads must reach. And to reach the t+1 barrier, y=0 must have finished the t+1 reduction. No race because y=1 wrote to page1 and waits; y=0 writes to page1 after its t reduction, then arrives. But y=1 might have also finished t+2 compute and is trying to write to page0... wait, y=1 can't get to t+2 because it's blocked at the t+1 barrier! So yes! With ONLY a barrier after the write phase (which we always need), double buffering ensures no races! Because: 1. The write phase for t uses page[t%2] 2. Barrier ensures all writes to page[t%2] are done 3. Reduction reads from page[t%2] 4. Meanwhile, threads can proceed to compute t+1 and write to page[(t+1)%2] 5. OK, let me now write the actual kernel. ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define D_STATE 16 __global__ void selective_scan_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ delta, const float* __restrict__ B, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ A_log, const float* __restrict__ D_param, float* __restrict__ y, int batch, int L, int d_inner) { const int d_state = D_STATE; const int d_idx = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 const int n = threadIdx.y; // 0..15 const int num_d_per_block = blockDim.x; // 32 const int bd_block = blockIdx.x * num_d_per_block; const int bd = bd_block + d_idx; const int d = bd % d_inner; const int b = bd / d_inner; if (bd >= batch * d_inner) return; const float A_val = -expf(A_log[d * d_state + n]); const float D_val = D_param[d]; float h = 0.0f; // Shared memory layout: [n][d_idx] extern __shared__ float smem[]; // smem[n * num_d_per_block + d_idx] for (int t = 0; t < L; ++t) { float x_val = x[(b * L + t) * d_inner + d]; float delta_val = delta[(b * L + t) * d_inner + d]; float B_val = B[(b * L + t) * d_state + n]; float C_val = C[(b * L + t) * d_state + n]; h = expf(delta_val * A_val) * h + x_val * delta_val * B_val; smem[n * num_d_per_block + d_idx] = h * C_val; __syncthreads(); if (n == 0) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < d_state; ++i) { sum += smem[i * num_d_per_block + d_idx]; } y[(b * L + t) * d_inner + d] = sum + x_val * D_val; } __syncthreads(); } } ``` Wait, I used `extern __shared__ float smem[];` but I need to allocate it in the kernel launch. The size is `num_d_per_block * d_state * sizeof(float) = 32 * 16 * 4 = 2048` bytes. In the kernel launch: ```cpp selective_scan_kernel<<<blocks, dim3(32, 16), 32 * 16 * sizeof(float)>>>(...); ``` Blocks calculation: ``` int num_d_per_block = 32; int total_d = batch * d_inner; int blocks = (total_d + num_d_per_block - 1) / num_d_per_block; ``` For batch=16, d_inner=512, total_d = 8192. blocks = 8192 / 32 = 256. One issue: what if `bd >= batch * d_inner`? For the last block, if total_d is not a multiple of 32, some threads might be out of bounds. We have an early return, but when threads in the same block diverge with `return`, __syncthreads() causes undefined behavior! Because not all threads in the block reach the sync point. To avoid this, we can: 1. Pad total_d to a multiple of 32. 2. Ensure the last block handles fewer d values but still has all threads participate in __syncthreads(). D_param[d] : 0.0f; float h = 0.0f; for (int t = 0; t < L; ++t) { float x_val = valid ? x[(b * L + t) * d_inner + d] : 0.0f; float delta_val = valid ? delta[(b * L + t) * d_inner + d] : 0.0f; float B_val = valid ? (In a warp of 16) We want lane 0 and lane 8 to have the correct sums. Standard: for offset=4,2,1: val += __shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset, 8) At offset=4: lane 0 reads lane 4: gets v4 lane 1 reads lane 5: gets v5 lane 2 reads lane 6: gets v6 lane 3 reads lane 7: gets v7 lane 4 reads lane 8: OUT OF GROUP 0! Gets v4 (self). lane 5 reads lane 9: OUT! Gets v5. lane 6 reads lane 10: OUT! Gets v6. lane 7 reads lane 11: OUT! Gets v7. lane 8 reads lane 12: valid in group 1. Gets v12. lane 9 reads lane 13: valid. Gets v13. lane 10 reads lane 14: valid. Gets v14. lane 11 reads lane 15: valid. Gets v15. lane 12 reads lane 16: OUT! Gets v12. ... After offset=4: lane 0 = v0 + v4 lane 8 = v8 + v12 At offset=2: lane 0 reads lane 2 = v2 + v6. Lane 0 = v0+v4+v2+v6. lane 2 reads lane 4 = v4+v4 = 2v4. Lane 2 becomes v2+v6+2v4. lane 8 reads lane 10 = v10+v14. Lane 8 = v8+v12+v10+v14. ... At offset=1: lane 0 reads lane 1 = v1+v5+v3+v7. Lane 0 = v0+v4+v2+v6+v1+v5+v3+v7. CORRECT! lane 8 reads lane 9. What is lane 9 at offset=1? After offset=2: lane 9 reads lane 11 = v11+v15. So lane 9 = v9+v13+v11+v15. After offset=1: lane 8 reads lane 9 = v9+v13+v11+v15. Lane 8 = v8+v12+v10+v14+v9+v13+v11+v15. CORRECT! So the result IS correct in lane 0 of each group! The corruption in other lanes doesn't propagate to lane 0 because lane 0 never reads a corrupted lane. Wait, in my previous analysis I said lane 16 reads lane 18 which is corrupted. Let me check lane 16 (group 1, lane 0). In the group of 16, lane 16 corresponds to index 0. It reads: - offset=8: index 8 (lane 24). Lane 24 at offset=8 reads lane 32 (out), gets v24. So lane 24 = v24+v24 = 2v24. But lane 16 reads v24. Wait, lane 16 reads lane 24 BEFORE lane 24 is corrupted. Because all reads happen simultaneously at the __shfl_down_sync instruction. At the moment of the read, lane 24's register still has the original v24. After the instruction, lane 24 adds its own value (v24) to itself, becoming 2v24. But lane 16 already got the original v24. So lane 16 = v16 + v24 (original). At offset=4: lane 16 reads lane 20. What is lane 20 at this moment? After offset=8, lane 20 read lane 28 (original v28). So lane 20 = v20 + v28. Lane 16 reads this. Lane 16 = v16 + v24 + v20 + v28. At offset=2: lane 16 reads lane 18. After offset=4, what is lane 18? At offset=8, lane 18 read lane 26 (original v26). So lane 18 = v18 + v26. At offset=4, lane 18 reads lane 22. After offset=8, lane 22 = v22 + v30. So lane 18 reads v22+v30. Lane 18 = v18 + v26 + v22 + v30. Lane 16 reads lane 18 = v18 + v26 + v22 + v30. Good! At offset=1: lane 16 reads lane 17. After offset=2, lane 17? At offset=8, lane 17 read lane 25. So lane 17 = v17 + v25. At offset=4, lane 17 reads lane 21. Lane 21 = v21 + v29. So lane 17 = v17 + v25 + v21 + v29. At offset=2, lane 17 reads lane 19. Lane 19 = v19 + v27 + v23 + v31? Wait: At offset=8, lane 19 read lane 27: v19 = v19 + v27. At offset=4, lane 19 reads lane 23. Lane 23 = v23 + v31. So lane 19 = v19 + v27 + v23 + v31. At offset=2, lane 17 reads lane 19 = v19 + v27 + v23 + v31. So lane 17 = v17 + v25 + v21 + v29 + v19 + v27 + v23 + v31. Then lane 16 reads lane 17 at offset=1. Lane 16 = v16 + v24 + v20 + v28 + v18 + v26 + v22 + v30 + v17 + v25 + v21 + v29 + v19 + v27 + v23 + v31. That's the sum of v16 through v31. CORRECT! Oh wow! So the standard shuffle reduction DOES work correctly for lane 0 and lane 16 of each subgroup! My earlier analysis about corruption was wrong because I didn't realize that at each level, the target lanes haven't been corrupted yet at the time of the read. The corruption happens after the read, but the reading lanes get the original (or correctly accumulated) values. This is a well-known fact: __shfl_down_sync with width and the standard reduction pattern works correctly for all subgroups, with the correct sum in the lowest lane of each subgroup. The "self-read" issue only affects the upper half of each subgroup, but those values are never read by the result lane. Phew! So I CAN use the warp shuffle approach! Now let me design the kernel using warp shuffle properly. Thread mapping: - Total threads = batch * d_inner * d_state = 131072 - blockDim = (16, 16) = 256 threads per block. This means: - threadIdx.x = n (0..15) - threadIdx.y = d_idx_in_block (0..15), so 16 (b,d) pairs per block - blockIdx.x determines the block of 16 (b,d) pairs. - Total blocks = 8192 / 16 = 512 blocks. Wait, with blockDim=(16, 16), threadIdx.x varies fastest. A warp is 32 threads = threadIdx.x=0..15, threadIdx.y=0..1. So each warp handles 2 (b,d) pairs, each with n=0..15. That's exactly what I wanted! For each block (16 pairs): - At time t, 16 warps compute in parallel (each warp is one pair). - Each thread computes h and val = h*C. - Within each warp, reduce across n using __shfl_down_sync(width=16). - After reduction, lane 0 of each half-warp writes y. Wait, but I said the warp handles 2 pairs. So after reduction: - lanes 0-15 reduce into lane 0 (for pair 0) - lanes 16-31 reduce into lane 16 (for pair 1) Both use __shfl_down_sync with width=16 on the same instruction. Then lane 0 and lane 16 write y. Memory access: - x[b,t,d]: broadcast within each half-warp - delta[b,t,d]: broadcast within each half-warp - B[b,t,n]: for fixed n, all threads in a warp load the same B. Broadcast! - C[b,t,n]: same, broadcast! - Wait, that's worse. If all 32 threads in a warp load the same B and C (since n is the same for all? No! threadIdx.x=n varies from 0 to 15 within each half-warp. Let me trace blockDim=(16, 16): thread ID = threadIdx.y * 16 + threadIdx.x Warp 0: - threads 0..15: threadIdx.y=0, threadIdx.x=0..15. n=0..15. - threads 16..31: threadIdx.y=1, threadIdx.x=0..15. n=0..15. For warp 0: - First half: d_idx=0, n=0..15 - Second half: d_idx=1, n=0..15 At time t: - Threads 0..15 load x[b,t,d0]. All same value -> broadcast. - Threads 16..31 load x[b,t,d1]. All same value -> broadcast. - Threads 0..15 load delta[b,t,d0]. Broadcast. - Threads 16..31 load delta[b,t,d1]. Broadcast. - Threads 0..15 load B[b,t,0..15]. Coalesced! (consecutive n values). - Threads 16..31 load B[b,t,0..15]. Coalesced! - Similarly for C. This is actually perfect! B and C are coalesced across the full warp (32 loads to adjacent addresses). x and delta are broadcast (2 loads). For y writes: - Lane 0 writes y[b,t,d0] - Lane 16 writes y[b,t,d1] Adjacent memory. But the warp only writes 2 values. Sparse. Total blocks = 512. 512 blocks * 16 warps = 8192 warps. 8192 / 132 SMs = ~62 warps per SM. That's very good! Wait, actually each block has 256 threads = 8 warps. 512 blocks * 8 warps = 4096 warps. 4096 / 132 = ~31 warps per SM. Good enough. This is much better than the shared memory approach because: 1. No __syncthreads() 2. Fewer memory transactions (no smem writes/reads) 3. Only 2 global stores per warp (sparse but total memory still just 64MB) I should use this approach! Let me write the kernel: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #define D_STATE 16 __global__ void selective_scan_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ delta, const float* __restrict__ B, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ A_log, const float* __restrict__ D_param, float* __restrict__ y, int batch, int L, int d_inner) { const int d_state = D_STATE; const int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; const int total_threads = batch * d_inner * d_state; if (tid >= total_threads) return; const int n = tid % d_state; const int bd = tid / d_state; const int d = bd % d_inner; const int b = bd / d_inner; const float A_val = -expf(A_log[d * d_state + n]); const float D_val = D_param[d]; float h = 0.0f; const int x_base = (b * L) * d_inner + d; const int state_base = (b * L) * d_state + n; const int y_base = (b * L) * d_inner + d; #pragma unroll 4 for (int t = 0; t < L; ++t) { const float x_val = x[x_base + t * d_inner]; const float delta_val = delta[x_base + t * d_inner]; const float B_val = B[state_base + t * d_state]; const float C_val = C[state_base + t * d_state]; h = expf(delta_val * A_val) * h + x_val * delta_val * B_val; float val = h * C_val; // Reduce within each half-warp (16 lanes) unsigned int mask = 0xFFFFFFFF; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset, 16); } const int lane = threadIdx.y * 16 + threadIdx.x; // lane in warp: 0..31 if (lane == 0 || lane == 16) { y[y_base + t * d_inner] = val + x_val * D_val; } } } ``` Wait, I also want to remove the `if (tid >= total_threads) return;` because it causes divergence with __shfl_down_sync. If some threads are inactive, the shuffle might not work correctly. But if total_threads is a multiple of block size, all threads are active. For blockDim = (16, 16), block size = 256 threads. total_threads = 131072. 131072 / 256 = 512 exactly. So all threads are active! No early return needed. I can remove the return statement. But for robustness, if the model might be called with different batch sizes, I should keep it. But keeping it with __shfl_down_sync is dangerous because inactive threads reduce the participating mask. If mask=0xFFFFFFFF includes inactive threads, __shfl_down_sync might return undefined for reads from inactive threads. Let me check: if a thread is predicated off due to `if (tid >= total_threads) return;`, then __shfl_down_sync might not work for other threads trying to read from it. But since we have exact divisibility, I'll just assert or remove the guard for safety. Actually, if batch * d_inner * d_state is always a multiple of 256 (which it is for batch=16, d_inner=512, d_state=16: 131072 / 256 = 512), then we can safely launch exactly 512 blocks with no partial block. But for safety, I could add a comment or just not use the guard. Let me also consider using `#pragma unroll 4` on the loop to help with instruction scheduling. The loop body is large, so unroll by 4 should give the compiler more scheduling freedom without exploding code size. Now, for the host code: ```cpp torch::Tensor selective_scan_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor delta, torch::Tensor B, torch::Tensor C, torch::Tensor A_log, torch::Tensor D_param) { int batch = x.size(0); int L = x.size(1); int d_inner = x.size(2); auto y = torch::empty_like(x); const int total_threads = batch * d_inner * D_STATE; const dim3 blockDim(D_STATE, 16); // 16 * 16 = 256 threads const int blocks = total_threads / (blockDim.x * blockDim.y); // Should be exact division selective_scan_kernel<<<blocks, blockDim>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), delta.data_ptr<float>(), B.data_ptr<float>(), C.data_ptr<float>(), A_log.data_ptr<float>(), D_param.data_ptr<float>(), y.data_ptr<float>(), batch, L, d_inner ); return y; } ``` I'll make sure all tensors are contiguous float32 before passing to the kernel. In PyTorch, I can add `.contiguous()` to ensure. Actually, the tensors coming from PyTorch ops like chunk, transpose, linear might already be contiguous or not. Let me ensure in the forward: ```python y = fused_scan( x_branch.contiguous(), delta.contiguous(), B_t.contiguous(), C_t.contiguous(), self.A_log.contiguous(), self.D.contiguous() ) ``` Wait, A_log and D are parameters, they should be contiguous. Now, is there anything else? One more optimization: I can fuse the z gate and residual add operations into custom kernels if they become bottlenecks. But let's see. Actually, I wonder if I should also write a fused kernel for the gate + out_proj + residual. The out_proj is a matmul which is best left to cuBLAS. But gate (y * silu(z)) is elementwise. For elementwise ops, PyTorch might not fuse them automatically in eager mode. I can write a small custom kernel or just rely on them being fast. With memory, maybe 0.1-0.2ms. Still small. conv1d and in_proj are also fast. So the bottleneck is definitely the scan, and my kernel should be a massive improvement. One more thing: I need to handle the fact that `proj_delta` has bias=True. PyTorch nn.Linear handles this automatically. And I should ensure the numerical tolerance is met. The scan kernel uses single-precision float. PyTorch reference also uses float. The main differences in numerical accuracy come from: 1. Different reduction order in the dot product. Instead of PyTorch's vectorized sum, I use a shuffle tree reduction. With 16 terms, the error difference is within fp32 rounding and should be < 1e-4. 2. `expf` might have slightly different accuracy than torch.exp. Both use standard IEEE exp approximations. The difference should be minimal. Actually, I should also consider: is `torch.exp` implementing the same algorithm as CUDA `expf`? Yes, PyTorch uses standard libm-like implementations for exp on GPU. The results should match within 1 ULP. For the A_log: I compute `-expf(A_log[d * d_state + n])`. PyTorch computes `-torch.exp(self.A_log)`. Same operation. One potential issue: in the reference, the loop does: ``` h = dA[:, t] * h + x_db[:, t] ``` where `dA = torch.exp(delta.unsqueeze(-1) * A.unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0))`. This computes `exp(delta * A)` for all (b,d,n,t) at once using vectorized exp. My kernel computes `expf(delta_val * A_val)` per thread. The operations are identical, but vectorized vs scalar might have tiny differences due to different instruction sequences. Within 1 ULP. Then `y_t = (h * C_t[:, t].unsqueeze(1)).sum(dim=-1)` computes dot product of h and C_t for each (b,d). PyTorch's sum uses tree reduction or pairwise reduction in registers. My kernel also uses tree reduction via shuffle. The main difference might be in the exact order of additions. Let me think about tolerance specifically: - We have a sequential scan over 2048 steps. - Each step does exp and FMA. - Floating point errors in the recurrence accumulate over 2048 steps. - The reference does the same recurrence in PyTorch, but using tensor operations. PyTorch's elementwise ops also accumulate rounding errors. - My kernel follows the exact same recurrence order. - The main difference is the dot product reduction order over 16 elements. For 16 elements, the maximum rounding error from different summation orders is bounded by ~16 * eps * max(|terms|). Eps for fp32 is ~1e-7, so error is ~1.6e-6 * max(|terms|). As long as the terms aren't pathologically large, this should be well within 1e-4 absolute tolerance. I think we're fine. Let me write the complete Python module. Wait, one more thought: can I also fuse the SiLU activation of `z` with the gate multiplication into the scan kernel? Actually, the kernel already reads all those. Adding z adds 64MB read. But saves 64MB write of intermediate y and 64MB read of y. Net savings: 64MB. reasoning
The issue with the correctness is in the shuffle reduction. The problem is that I have a 2D block of 16x16, but I'm using `__shfl_down_sync` with a warp size of 16, but my threads are organized in a 2D grid where the total block size is 256 (16x16). The result in lane N is sum of lanes N, N^offset1, N^offset1^offset2, ... for all bitwise combinations. But we only want the sum in lane 0. For this to work correctly, we need width >= d_state, and all d_state lanes to have valid values.
